=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
[i]tl;dr - Samurai asks tough questions of you as you help yourself, but also help your neighbor. Who will win greater influence over medieval Japan? It all depends on the players. . .[/i]
[COLOR=#CCCCFF]____________________________[/COLOR]

Let's talk about Samurai for a minute. Brilliant abstract? Check. Tense decisions that affect everyone else around the table? Check. A simple ruleset with a lot of depth? Check. Beautiful plasticy/clacky playing pieces? Checkity check check (oh I love to hear them rattle around in my hand).

Samurai is a simple game where you are trying to surround three types of figures: high hats (military), rice paddies (farming), and buddhas (priesthood). These three aspects of Japanese society will generally represent your influence/power over Japan, but let's not kid anyone: this is an abstract. That's totally fine, though, because this has the well-seasoned draw of a game that's confident in its own success and knows, just knows, that it will suck you in. 

So how do you get these pieces? You get them by surrounding their spots on a hex map of Japan. When a piece is totally surrounded by player hexes, the relative power of those hexes is compared, and whoever has the highest power for that symbol takes the piece. In other words, we all have hexes that range from 2-4 power, for each symbol, and as we gradually fill up the board, we're gauging one another's power around the various cities. 

The game ends when every piece has been removed from the board. At that point, we lift our player screens and reveal how many pieces of each type we've collected. If one player has a majority in two types of pieces, they win the game immediately. Otherwise, the game goes to a tiebreaker. Players remove all pieces from the type they won, and then compare the number of remaining pieces: most pieces wins. This is a brilliant little piece of strategy since your strongest power will be removed in the case of a tiebreaker -- so you need to be sure to be strong in all three areas. 

Samurai is honestly simpler to play than explain (I made a real hash of it above, but the actual act of playing is very simple), but it has some deviously difficult strategy. For one thing, we all have a few pieces that bend the rules of the game -- and we all have the same few pieces, but the timing of when we play them is the challenge! Waiting until the other players have used their valuable 'swap' hex makes your remaining swap hex the most valuable move left in the game -- but on the other hand, you could really swap those two tiles, and ahhhh, I can't wait! I'll swap it now. 

In summary, Samurai is a fantastic game that will see you bumping up against your friends on a too-tight map of Japan. It plays in about 30 minutes with 2-3 players, and can also accommodate 4 thanks to some clever modular boards. Highly recommended, you owe it to yourself to check out this classic.

2016-02-08
Samurai is a pure abstract and the theme is there to only give the players some vague connection between numbers and real life. Instead of assigning a "three" you assign three "samurai". Samurais represent power which is easy to understand, and this is the only thing along with the map of Japan that connect the game with reality. Of course, it is not always fair to criticise a board game for its lack of reality, since you have to bring things to an abstract level to make a board game in the first place. However, for some reason with Samurai I found the game and the theme were disconnected. 

But once I was able to get past that, the game showed its true worth. Every turn you are facing tough decisions, which is something I particularly enjoy. There is enough luck in the tile draw to keep things interesting through introducing some fluctuation in the balance of power. Most of the time your decisions are tactical aiming at short to intermediate term goals, but there is a long term strategy element to the game as well. Scoring is such that it makes all fights over territory meaningful.

Samurai may not be the most exciting game out there but it works, and most importantly is fun if you like tactical games and do not mind direct competition.

RATING: 8.57
Samurai was reprinted this year. It is an abstract game by Reiner Knizia. I only like abstract games that look great and Samurai is beautiful. It is a very clever tile-laying game where each player places his tiles on a Japanese grid to gain majority by adjacency over three different types of statues. The scoring is tricky because if you tie for one type of statue you count all your other statues as tie-breaker. I wish the game box would have a smaller size.

Players: 2-4
Time: 30-60m

RATING: 7
This is not as good as Tigris and Euphrates, obviously.  It's also, I think, a lesser game than Through the Desert.  That said, I like it a lot -- it's fast, interesting, tense, a lot of things I want in a game with this length.  2 player, it's a fine 20-30 minutes.

My wife (and a lot of other people) think it's "ok" -- perhaps dull, perhaps confusing, perhaps too arithmetic-ish.  They have a point.  My primary gripe is that the scoring is rather baroque and non-intuitive, while Tigris is perfection and Through the Desert makes sense in a component-balancing way.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Samurai is my first Knizia game and I thoroughly enjoyed the game mechanics and design. It's a short, quick, simple game with a subtle strategy. Yet, the moves can be complex and thought-provoking with a chess-like quality. 

One of my favorite elements is the game end trigger - the game ends when the last figure of any type is removed from the board, or when the 4th figure is placed beside the board due to a tie. So, if you think you're in the lead, you can force the game to end. Now, there are 3 types of figures on the game board: high helmets, rice fields, and budhas. If you get the most figures of 2-3 types at the game end, you win. If there is a tie, the end scoring is complicated and the rule book doesn't properly explain (to new players anyway) how the winner is determined. Even now that I understand the tie conditions, it's not entirely intuitive.

My bf has several issues with the game. He strongly dislikes the end scoring, particularly since your strategy is fairly limited and predetermined by the 5 face up tiles hidden behind your player screen. He feels that because you don't have much choice over which particular figure to go for (that you can possibly win), the end scoring shouldn't be tied to getting the most figure types only (with no value associated to the number of figures). His suggestion is that there should be a point system - something like: score 3 pts for each figure that you have the most of (compared to your other pieces), score 2 pts for the second most, and 1 pt for the third. I personally don't have a problem with the end scoring in Samurai, but I do agree with the overall analysis. However, since most of our games are with 2 players, maybe the game dynamic will change with more players. 

UPDATE: This is another favorite game in my collection.

RATING: 7
Finally got around to playing this classic, and it did not disappoint.  This is a well designed spatial challenge with some excellent player prediction concerns.  I say this after playing the one time.  

The one thing that causes me some concern with Samurai is that perhaps games can be won or lost simply based upon whether you properly memorized which token each player took.  I would hate to see that becoming the core, decisive factor in such an otherwise well-designed game.  I wonder how the game would play if "tokens taken" were visible for all to see.  Hmmm... (8 of 10, August 2013)

On second play, not sure I would rate it quite so highly due to the essentially non-existent theme, going down to a 7, and as before, the memorization of tokens should likely be scuttled from the game design, such that as they are acquired they are simply placed in front of the screens. (7 of 10, September 2014)

The game affords the strategist ample opportunity to think and rethink plans.  The scoring mechanism forces the players to balance scoring a majority in one symbol and capturing as many other symbols as possible.  No majority score eliminates the player from contention.  Too few other symbols losses the game.  [GTI Code: AGSD]

RATING: 7
Samurai is a tactical/abstract game where players are placing titles to win faction tokens (only 3 types). If a player manages to have majority in 2 or 3 factions at the end of the game, they win. Thing is... that's unlikely to happen. If there is a tie for majority, each player with a majority counts the other faction token they control and whoever has the most wins. Thus players want to have majorities but they don't necessarily want more tokens than they need of that particular majority. And finding that balance of faction tokens is partially what makes this game so rich. 

The other part is the limited distribution of tiles, which players place to win those faction tokens. Each player has the same tiles and will never place tokens back into the draw bag. Thus, as tiles are placed, players have concrete information to make very informed decisions about what can be placed. Thus, a player may ignore taking a faction token (which happens when it is surrounded on land) knowing they can't lose it. But the player must also be mindful of end-game conditions, as another player may attempt to end the game so that faction token remains unresolved. 

In some ways, this game can be seen as a lighter version of Tigris and Euphrates. But through its simple rule set, it manages to have multiple layers of depth and feels quite chess-like in its calculating/abstract nature. Overall, it is a brilliantly designed game and I can see my rating increase with more plays. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
A wonderful tile placement game from Renier Knizia. The components are top notch, from the puzzle piece boards to the hex tiles and the plastic figurines. The only downsides are that the boards are a bit on the thin side and the '1', stylized to look like Sinic character writing like many things set in East Asia, looks like a '7', which can be confusing to new players.

The game play is very simple and surprisingly fast. Lay all the figurines on to the cities, with no two same types of figures on the same city, one at a time in turn order. Afterwards, just place a tile, take another turn if it has a kanji on it, then refresh to five tiles. If a city is completely surrounded by land tiles, the players with the most powerful tiles (indicated by the number on the tile) wins the figurine. As I said, it's very easy to explain and quick to play, although that is adjustable depending on how AP-tolerant players are (that a plus and minus, if the players agree on not). 

The biggest problem is with end game victory conditions. It's not that hard, but it's still on the wonky side. It's bit like Lost Cities in that way. It's best when explaining the game, to put this first and foremost. Definitely worth getting.

RATING: 8
Amazingly quick yet fun in a way that I'm not sure how.  It's a nim game of sorts, and perhaps works on the same principles as 6 Nimmt! and Coloretto, though it uses a board and hexagonal placement and one of Knizia's signature scoring methods.
  
I keep this game around because it's simple, quick, elegant, and a beautiful demonstration of Knizia's scoring method.  It also plays well with the number of players that it advertises (as Knizia games typically do).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This game has limited luck involved. You initially choose your opening hand of tiles, and replace every tile you use randomly from your set of tiles. However, having random tiles isn't always that random, because for every tile there are only so many locations you intend to place it. Some of the game is having the right tile handy at the right opportune moment, but a good player can always make the most of what he has, while holding that perfect tile (or combo of tiles) until the moment is right to strike. For those with good memories, you can usually work out what tiles the opponents have remaining as well. So it can boil down to maximising your effect versus theirs, which makes it more the game of skill. For just a little bit of chaos, there are two tiles which let you switch something on the board - either re-use one of your great tiles, or swap two of the trophy pieces across the board. And some pieces can be played two or more per turn, which keeps you on your toes.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A Knizia abstract title with a quaint Japanese theme that employs area control and set collecting mechanics. 

I've introduced this to non gamers with positive results; the simple rules coupled with the pleasing production values being the primary factor in their enjoyment. The endgame scoring could be taken as a little obtuse.

This is not to say that the game is overly simplistic. There is a lot going on and the more experienced player will inevitably win.

This is a solid, elegant game that scales well with just the right mix of luck that is great for gamers and non gamers alike.

RATING: 6
Very simple, very clever, but with an annoyingly complex scoring system. One thing I do NOT like about this game (this is a general pet peeve of mine) is that it has a setup round (placing the figures on the board) that presupposes a deeper knowledge of the game. Even after playing the game once, I don't think my wife or I knew enough to have ANY sense whatsoever about how best to place the initial figures. And I have a sense that that initial placement has a big effect on the eventual outcome.

That being said, the game itself has this sort of classical Knizia tension where you build something up slowly, increasing the tension until suddenly it breaks, barely in favor of one player or another. And there's the trademark feature of wanting to place tiles in a half-dozen places -- without the resources to do so!

UPDATE: 7 -> 6. This game is too abstract and brain-burning.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Updated 8/20/10.
5+ plays.  All 2 players. 

I've now played Samurai quite a bit, and the game has grown on my somewhat from my initial few plays.  The last two plays were especially tense and competitive.  I've moved it up to the [u]very[/u] bottom end of my sevens.  

One of the problems with approaching the game as a new player is that it lacks the required interstitial game states to provide in-game feedback.  When I play a tile to the board, nothing tells me whether I've done well or poorly.  Even my play results in capturing the token I'd been eyeing, I am not immediately conscious of the opportunity costs of my play (which play a large role in successful strategy).  Consequently, the game has a quite high entry barrier, which, I think, really undermines some of the fun to be had.  Better players will almost inevitably win, and poor players will rarely understand why they lost without many repeated plays.  For a game that runs 30 minutes, and in which most of your time is spent deep in thought, the underwhelming sense produced by the endgame is disappointing.

But between two evenly matched opponents, Samurai is a fun challenge that adds a different (spatial) dimension to Knizia's number-crunching offerings (Battle Line, Lost Cities, and Keltis).  The components are attractive and top-notch.  I'm not sure that the endless replayability is really there, but if you only take it out for two games a month, it will probably keep you entertained for quite some time.

Wife:9
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Very Knizia-esque. That was my first reaction after having played this game.

Samurai lacks the conflicts seen in E&T, which offer so much excitement and dynamism to board development - the ebb and flow of empires. For a game set in war-torn Feudal Japan, it's for my tastes too tame and peaceful. In terms of mechanics, I consider E&T's theme more credible.

Similarly, I find Samurai more calculational in nature compared to E&T since you're constantly sumning up the various numbers printed on tiles. Not quite what one would expect from what many here describe as the lighter counterpart to E&T.

Okay, Samurai might not compare favourably with Knizia's Opus Magnum, but what about the other tile-laying games of his?

Samurai and Through the Desert roughly equal each other in terms of playing time. However, I should argue that the latter is more accessible and enjoyable to "non-gamers". Less calculational and more intuitive scoring (that said, I do like Samurai's manner of scoring).

Both Trough the Desert and Samurai score points for scaling better than E&T, it has to be said.

Einfach Genial is a more recent entry into Knizia's tile-laying series and may be the lightest of them yet, though the fun factor of camel-laying Through the Desert appears to me greater.

Here my list of preference:
1) E&T
2) Through the Desert
3) Samurai
4) Einfach Genial

All perfectly enjoyable and as tastes differ, so will other people rank these in an other manner.

Oh yes, Klear Games has done a very nicely done PC conversion. Well worth checking out!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Context free, I would rate this game higher. It's a very fun game. I like figuring out how to place my tiles. Having little short term plans with the little knowledge I have.

However, that last sentence is the problem. I really enjoy the random draw in Tigris & Euphrates. It creates the element of uncertainty that adds risk to engaging conflict. However, it does not prevent long term planning. In Samurai I felt more that until the end game when my pool of tiles was tiny or empty, that long term planning was a bit of a fool's errand. Too many unknowns (both mine and my opponent's).

Which brings me to a swing in the other direction. Through the Desert is also about claiming spots on the board, but in this one there is no hidden information. It allows me to plan into the future, but more importantly, it allows me to make decisions knowing that I'm sacrificing one area for another. In Samurai, with all the hidden information, I never know if I'm sacrificing anything by not playing somewhere. I feel I am playing blindly.

Thus, Samurai is a game I enjoy playing, but would almost always choose either T&E or TtD over it.

RATING: 9
A near zero-luck game that's pretty deep that plays in an hour. Nice. The production value is very high without being cheesy, a eurogame rarity. 

I don't usually have a strong opinion on hidden trackable information, but this is so much better and so much more tense with open holdings. 

Playing this again recently gave me such a feeling of delight that is absent from the grindhouse of contemporary Euros that I had to move it up from 8 to 9. It took me exactly 3 minutes to explain the rules to a new player, and played in 45 minutes with 4 players. It's all about manipulating your opponents into placing in the areas you want them to - a quality absent these days. Outstanding game.

RATING: 9
Extremely clever gameplay - the good Doctor, Knizia, strikes again! I absolutely love this game. My girlfriend on the other hand... Not so much. However, luckily my father enjoys this game tremendously and is almost always willing to give it a whirl. My rating is for 2-3 players; with 4, it's more like a 6 as it's much more chaotic... 

It plays pretty quickly compared to the deep gameplay you get, so it's easy to squeeze this one in between other games - it's probably the BEST short game (short as in 30-45 minutes or so) I've ever played - although even I can get AP in this game, and I'm definitely not an "AP-kinda-guy".

The fact that you draw your tiles randomly has decreased my rating slightly as I have seen several times that it's VERY hard to win if you for instance start with the swap tiles (both the "figure swap" tile and the "swapping your old placed tile for this" tile) along with some samurais. And if you get your Japanese rider and the ships in the end, well, then it's going to be much harder to win. Also, there is a strong right-player binding as you can benefit a lot if the player to your right makes a suboptimal move, and the first player mathematically seems to have an advantage, but it hasn't been too big of a problem in my plays.

However, that being said, a skilled player will beat a new player almost every time (like in Puerto Rico and such). With equally skilled players, it's a great tactical game and it's definitely one of my favorite games. Once again Knizia has created a masterpiece!

RATING: 8
Received via a math trade by trading away Neuroshima Hex. After reading the rules and playing a "dummy" game, I can tell that I significantly upgraded one nasty tile laying game for another. This has a lot of promise.

Update: rated 8 after two back-to-back plays. Yep, I like this better than Neuroshima Hex. There's greater tension, and though there's the luck of the tile draw, it's easier to mitigate some bad draws. There's always something to do with your five tiles.

Reiner Knizia's best games incorporate tense and tough choices. This is right up there with his best games.

Update: Two more plays with family. Quick observation--this is not a gateway game. Although the rules are streamlined, the tactics/strategy is pretty obtuse for a casual gamer. That doesn't mean it's a bad game; I think it's a very solid game. It's simply not easy on the casual gamer.

Update: I ultimately sold this. I really like the game, but I've not found other players that share my opinion. If this gets reprinted I can see myself picking it up again. But for now, I'll maintain my collection and ensure I'm keeping games that get played.

RATING: 9
80plus plays (4p, 3p, 2p). More plays not logged here. Engaging, tense, easy rules, beautiful mechanism, tricky, anxious strategy--a lot of drama and nail-biting, opportunity for tricky plays too. It looks great, and its design clarity helps the play. In my experience, sometimes leads to nearly silent play, but not in all groups. Quite fun yet thinky game, highly recommended, really a must-own, in my opinion. The scoring makes for surprise game plots. There is a bit of luck, but on average seems like a controlled amount to keep it approachable (moreso with Select Starting Tiles, although I prefer Random) and freedom from determination while still rewarding brain calculation.  It may be that the endgame is sometimes Solvable (notwithstanding unpredictability of 3, 4 player games.)  Scales exceptionally well... however I think the 2 player game is best (2 player rating 9; 3-4 players I'd rate an 8).  Kingmaker endings are possible, but not a huge problem.  3 or 4 players may suffer sometimes from the "unfairness" feeling of some multi-player wargames and area control games (why pick on me, I'm not the leader, you are giving Player X to your left the victory, etc). However, play can be rapid and that is a cure for that problem. Most of my plays are 4-player.  And here is something special-- a friend called me up from across the country to thank me for introducing him to the game. I think Samurai a classic. It packs flat in a large plastic envelope. UPDATE: playing many games on the iTouch/iPad app with faraway pals.  After several years Samurai remains the eurogame that I play the most.

RATING: 8
I can;t quite put my finger on what I feels so elegant about the game, but it has struck a nerve with me.  It is a simple game of tile management and area control.  Usually these kinds of game only appeal to me if there is a layered component of area control (like Carolus Magnus or King of Siam), but this one plays so smooth.

There are some nice elements in the game that help you control tempo, which turns what could be a blandly tactical  into something that feels a little more strategic.  The coming of the end game can be controlled to an extent, and "fast" tiles can be used to deliver a 1-2 punch (or 1-2-3 punch) to swing some key scoring in your direction.  The system works pretty well to provide constraints in the limited number of tiles you hold, and the tie-breaker scenario creates a bit of tension in the 2-player game.

RATING: 7
A very good game.  Scales very well from 2-4 players.  Doesn't have the problem with three players that most games do, which is that two players square off and leave the third to win handily.  Though it's subtle, conflict actually benefits the winner more than an unopposed capture would.  Losing a conflict, however, is a terrible setback, so a wise player is careful to never overcommit.  The entire game is designed to reward using the minimum required force to acheve the desired effect.  

The extra gravy is that Samurai clocks in at under an hour.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Yet another tile laying title from Knizia. More to think about than Durch but less control. Much less convoluted than Euphrat. An interesting medium between the two. The basic idea being to surround scoring pieces (3 different kinds) with the highest valued hexes possible denoting that piece, thusly gaining a majority and capturing the piece. The goal being to gain the most control in at least 1, preferably 2 of the sets. Because that can be difficult to do with 3 players, the game has a nice tie break mechanism as well which rewards the player who captured the most pieces outside of the suit that they won, thus rewarding diversity. The random tile draw is a bit ugly at times, but in a game this short some luck is acceptable to me. Overall decent, but I don't like it more than Durch despite the variance in tile value because the randomness of the draw makes it frustrating at times while one always is in complete control in Durch. Bland.

RATING: 6
Samurai is one of the last 'olde skool' titles published by 999 Games which I hadn't played, so when the opportunity presented itself somewhere in 2005 or 2006, I was very eager to give it a go. I don't remember much from the experience, save that the scoring sounded complex and confusing.

Fast forward to 2009. With a gaming neighbour's copy we decided to play a few games with 3 players. The scoring sounded very clear and straightforward now, and I was able to focus much better on what needed doing. The game turned out to be a sort of area influence title, with as prize stylish black playing pieces, and the winner having the highest total of pieces collected next to an absolute majority of a single type. In other words, collect pieces of one type like crazy until you have the majority (which in a 3 player game is likely with 4, extremely likely with 5, and assured with 6), and then collect as many of the other two types as possible. Really not that complex.

As for the game... Well, it allowed for a bit of interesting tactical play, but overall I found it bland and a bit boring. You don't have much control over your own influence, and one more than one occasion a carefully constructed soon-to-be-majority around a playing piece was upset by a strong tile of another player, or a move-stuff-about one. This blind aspect makes it less interesting than it should be; I think I'd like it a lot more if the game were played open, so without hidden information. But then it becomes a direct competitor of Through the Desert, which in my opinion has more interesting game play: that game is about encirclement and not about multiple majorities around certain fields on the board. Samurai is by no means a bad game, but I do not consider it 'great' either. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.5
My 2nd eurogame. 

Compared to Puerto Rico, this was a lot simpler to learn the rules of. 

I felt a bit annoyed that the screen doesn't list the numbers of each unit type - having that information would have helped me immediately know the relative strength of each number without need for familiarisation or memory issues.

As with the 'hidden' information in Puerto Rico and Taj Mahal, I could imagine a serious player trying to remember the scores. Part of me felt like I SHOULD be remembering all this - it felt unnatural to have game-pertinent information at my fingertips and just 'wilfully' forget it.

Though I agree with Ian that - having forgotten the scores - the scoring method adds some tension, I'd definitely try to remember them in a later game if playing to win.

Despite the inherent randomness of 3+ player games, the shuffled cards and group politics, it felt like a fairly 'pure' strategic game to me. It felt a lot closer to Blokus or Draughts than to something like Puerto Rico.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Tile laying game of majority scoring. Three scoring categories (rice, Buddhas, and helmets), all represented by pieces on the board. When all land spaces surrounding one or more pieces are filled with tiles, pieces are awarded to the player with the most points on tiles of the corresponding scoring category, but only the tiles adjacent to that space count. Object is to score most of at least two of the three categories. And then some other tie breakers that push players to get some of the other scoring categories as well. 

I find it to be a decent game, but not great. There are some special tiles that you can play extra on your turn which can really change things up, but ultimately, it's just a tile-laying game and a little too abstract for me to like more. No other real mechanics to explore here.

RATING: 8
[after two 4 player and one 3 player game] I finally got to play this game last night and it was a blast.  It was very tense throughout.  I'm not very good at keeping track of how many of each piece each player has captured and I was surprised to find that I had won the game with 4-3-3 (2nd place had 4-3-2).  The game seemed very tactical to me.  My plans evolved as the game progressed.  I started out by focusing on the Buddhas, but toward the end of the game the High Hats became available so I started capturing those and ended up with a majority of them.  The special tiles also add some interesting twists to the game.  I hope I can get more plays of this game in soon.  It looks like it scales from 2 to 4 players well because of the modular board.  Pretty simple game to explain, too.  It's likely that my rating will go up rather than down with more plays. [update 2/10/06: after 3 plays I find no reason to lower my rating, would play this again pretty much anytime.]

RATING: 9
Samurai is like a thematically rich, diceless wargame.  I find that it encourages a sense of quiet contemplation; from the serenity of the woodblock print style of the artwork to the ebb and flow of the gameplay as tiles are committed and sacrificed and thrusts are made to capture critical pieces, I think it perfectly captures the idea of a struggle for power through support of the castes in medieval Japan.  The reason I don't rate this a 10 is the fact that a weak player often adds unpredictabile chaos to what is otherwise a very analytical game (though, I suppose, the same could be said of many games).

RATING: 6
Those who say that any Knizia creation is a scoring system looking for a game must have had Samurai in mind. This is a pedestrian area influence game during which your chief strategy is to avoid setting up another player --- until you get to the final scoring, which is odd and favors players with better memories. Odd scoring works if a game's nature requires it, but it just seems arbitrary here. That's a long way of saying that I think this game is overrated. It probably merits a five, but I bump it to six because of the outstanding production.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
An awesome and incredibly nerve wracking game.

IMO this game is kind of like an inverse version of Go; Go is a game of efficient plays where true skill is found is setting up areas to fall with the least amount pieces and direct attention. Samurai is a game where once you start contesting an area, you had better see it through to completion before moving on, lest the resource get captured by your opponent(s). In samurai, once a resource has been taken, there is no getting back. 

A particularly nerve wracking dimension is knowing your opponents have exactly the same tools at their disposal as you, including all the same screwage tiles. Each game is always anticipating some crushing disappointment somewhere. 

Finally, with more and more players, the amount of time between turns becomes unbearable as you see your good plays thwarted and canceled by the other players moves.

I love this game.

RATING: 8
Clever tile laying game from Reiner Knizia.  Everyone plays with an identical tile set, but with a limited tile pool.  The tiles are played to try and build up majorities of influence around victory tokens, but you only qualify for victory if you score an outright majority in one of the spheres.  Clever stuff and one that requires a bit of thought.

RATING: 9
Fantastic. It blends the strategic feeling of a perfect-information board game with the tactical feeling of an information-limiting card game. It maintains steady tactical, strategic, and endgame tension. Every one of your 20 pieces feels valuable. The pressure to slowly dominate the spaces around what you want is counteracted by the opportunity to quickly fill the last spaces around other players' nearly-surrounded pieces. Packs some satisfying thinking into an hour or less.

RATING: 6.5
Clever game with a great look, even if the theme is rather tacked-on. Plays best with 2 or 3. Can suffer from analysis paralysis if the mood or players are wrong. 

The victory conditions are awful and some gray areas exist as written. Nothing like engaging in a long, grueling game of wits only to engage in a discussion on rules interpretation to decide who won. 

Also, this is the type of game where the more time you spend staring at the board, the better you'll do. In general, I dislike these types of games. In this one, you'll need to compute every possible move your opponents might make based on the pieces they have not played yet (which means you have to spend time scanning the board to see what's already in play) in order to do well. 

In brief, I still like this game, but I think it's overrated.

RATING: 8
Samurai is an interesting game where you try to capture statues. Collecting statues is fun. You try to get the majority. There are 3 kinds of statues.

Deciding on who wins in Samurai is neat too. The are a lot of tie breakers which is good.

Samurai is one of my very first euro games in my board game collection. One of the other very first euro games is San Marco.

The Ronin tile can cause a surprise for a fast closing and multiple captures of statues. Especially since it's the only land tile which lets you put two land tiles on the same turn. 

Boats are also interesting for giving support to cities. It can be a difference if they are played right.

The exchange of statue and exhange of tile can really be nasty for someone at times. It is also fun to see someone's reaction whenever that happends during the game. Someone can really make the game turn around with these two tiles. Screwing a player with these two tiles can be easy.


8 plays since recording games played.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
This has been one of the most played games in my collection over the years and I suspect that if I were to find the house on fire and I have a minute to grab an armful of games this would be the first one I'd reach for to save.

Why is this getting a 10?  It's my favorite of the Knizia's tile laying trilogy.  I'm not all that fond of E&T, and TtD, while I enjoy, just doesn't have that little extra something for me that Samurai has.

I suppose this is the perfect representative for a german game for me.  It scales well from 2-4 players, it plays in a fairly short period of time, the game system is elegant and induces painful decision making for the players, and it has quality game components that evoke german "exellence".

In terms of gameplay it hits the right spot for my mind.  Hard decision making, but it doesn't go so far that I feel I'm doing a brain burner.  There is just enough information for me to make good guesses on what to do next and how to react to other's play, but not so much that I'd need to be a savant to compete.

In the end I feel I get a quality gaming experience from Samurai.  It's still got lets after all these years and hundreds of other games in my collection to distract me.  I've yet to introduce it to someone who had anything but praise for the experience.

RATING: 8
Samurai is a very elegant game that like many successful games, is simple in play, yet deep in tactics.  With two players, the winner usually does so by capturing the majority in two categories, so the quirkiness of the scoring system is less of an issue.  With more than two, the scoring system at first seems very obtuse and almost random, especially at first. With more experience though, the scoring actually becomes an integral part of the strategy and you need to carefully watch the other players' captured pieces and adjust your plans

What makes this game shy of a full 10 points for me, is the lack of flexibility in the set up.  This creates a small sense of sameness, especially with the competition for Edo.  Nevertheless Samurai is a great game.

Update:  I've played it quite a bit and I love it.  I prefer it to Through the Desert and Tigris.  To me it's not as dry as those two and is much prettier. Of course I'm in this hobby for the bits and boards as well and Samurai is a distinctive looking game with great components.  I like the random tile draw, and enjoy the dash of randomness that exists in so many great Knizia games like Samurai and Tigris.

RATING: 8.5
I was hesitant to pick this one up after reading that this game suffered from "boxes" syndrome. (The game with the dots and you connect them to make boxes. Another winner from Knizia and a worthy entry into the tile laying trilogy. Highly recommended.

RATING: 8
[Tile-laying, Set collection, Time] [2P:7, 3P:9, 4P:8] Reiner likes to do all sorts of things with tiles and time. His 3-part Tile Laying Trilogy is exquisite in that each has time as an overarching theme to the mechanisms, but none of them implement it in quite the same way. Durch die Wuste, Through the Desert, plays time as opportunity. Do it now, and be certain, or do it later and hope no one blocks you. Euphrat & Tigris ties time with its tiles - the more tiles you burn through the less time you have until the game ends. So more war, more revolutions, less time. Samurai is the waiting game. Patience melds with positioning to produce situations where your opponents have no choice but to do your will and play their tiles where you want them to. It's just a matter of time. The brilliant, austere graphic design choices add to the atmosphere of the game, which implements several very Japanese tenets to enhance the gameplay. The basic mechanism of encirclement of cities, towns and villages to capture influence is very much in the spirit of Japanese strategy. The very simple rules - play exactly one regular tile and as many quick (shock troop) tiles as you wish on your turn - make the game a pleasure to teach. The thematic "ninja" tiles are simple and yet powerful. And that power is most embodied in the 1-power Ronin, the most fearsome unit at each Daimyo's disposal. The much-discussed scoring rules and winning conditions fit the game like a glove. Broad support from all three spheres of influence is necessary to win. And yes, ties are possible. There is no shame in a battle where you meet someone who is your equal. All in all, this is Reiner at his best - Samurai is a game that is thematic, beautiful, easy to learn, always interesting to play and provides immense replay value. Highly recommended. (And yes, I do like Samurai more than I like Durch die Wuste.)

RATING: 7
O tabuleiro é formado por ilhas definidas por uma malha hexagonal. Nós devemos colocar nossos marcadores (hexágonos) de influência em volta das cidades. Estes marcadores têm números e tipos que representam a força e tipo de inflência deles. Cada cidade tem um, dois ou três tipos de itens. Quando a cidade for cercada por marcadores de influência quem tiver mais força em cada um desses itens recebe os itens que definirão o vencedor no final do jogo. Basicamente disputa de área. O jogo parece ser bem legal. Nós jogamos com algumas regras erradas que, tenho certeza, mudaram bastante a graça do jogo. Na próxima eu confirmo. Não posso deixar de comentar o material e a qualidade gráfica da edição brasileira que deixou a desejar mas ainda assim estou pensando em comprá-lo.

RATING: 7
Nagyon jól kitalált, egyszerű ám mégis gondolkodós tile placement, területfoglalós játék. 
A játékban a térlképen lávő városok körülépítése a feladat. Amikor egy város körülépült, akkor kiértékelődik. A várost az a játékos  szerzi meg, akinek a város típusához illeszkedő befolyása a nagyobb a város körül. A játék végén az nyer, akinek a lehető legöbb várostípusból többsége lesz (nála lesz a legtöbb, abból a típusból). Kb. ennyi a szabály, és a játék mégis nagyon szórakoztató.

Két negatívumot tudok megemlíteni:
1. a hajók megtalálása a táblán nagyon nehézkes, mert nem üt el elégggé a színük.
2. Az hogy titkos, hogy kinek milyen városa van szerintem egy kicsit felesleges memóriajátékos elemet visz a játékba. De ezt még egy játék után, azért nem merem biztosan állítani. :)

-------------
Pár új játék után (2 személyes) kicsit több szerencse szerepet érzek már benne. Ha valaki az elején felhúzza a hármas samurait és még valami kisebbeket, akkor nagyon nehezen behozható előnyre tesz szert, mivel jó eséllyes elviszi a teljes hármas települést.

8.5 -> 7

Managing your tiles, including you starting tiles, is very fun.

RATING: 7
SAMURAI stammt aus einer Ära, in der Spiele von Dr. Knizia zum Besten gehörten, was man als Vielspieler damals auf den Tisch bekam: Zeug wie AMUN-RE, TADSCH MAHAL und eben SAMURAI gehören auch heute noch zu Spielen, die problemlos gealtert das heutige Kompliziert- und Komplexniveau mit einer genialem Idee gekonnt unterlaufen. SAMURAI ist ein regeleinfaches Mehrheit- und Werteplättchenlegespiel um drei Figurenarten aus haptischer Anmut auf einer gebogenen, himmelblauen, in cremefarbener Schönheit an Inseln reichen Japankarte mit einer intelligenten Schlusswertung, die Taktik in einen seidenen Kimono kleidet. Vermutlich würde man SAMURAI heute weniger oppulent bei einem Kleinverlag in eine kleine Schachtel verpacken und es würde immer noch als gutes Insider-Spielchen seine Freunde finden. SAMURAI verdient es öfters auf den Tisch zu kommen, als es sein Alter vorgaukelt. Danke für 20 gute Jahre. Wir schreiten alle einer senioren Gesellschaft entgegen.

RATING: 7
I have to admit the end game of this is one of my favorites.  It's a wonderful feeling looking across the table when you have won and announce yourself as soverign.  The game itself is an interesting area control struggle.  The components add a lot to the feel, and the game play is quick and tense.  Not my favorite of Knizia, but a solid title.

UPDATE: Time changes a lot, and it has too with my perception of Samurai.  About a decade ago Knizia was making the best games out there, and had an impressive string of titles that still really have no equal.  My many years now of playing Euros has lead me back to the start.  I believe the late nineties, and very early days of the new millenium to be the golden days of Euro design, and Knizia was at the helm.  Games today have become too convoluted, too over wrought, and lacking in real game play.  There are a few out there but they tend to imitate the earlier designs.  Recent playings of Samurai have proven to me the amazing game that it truly is.  Depth and simplicity, and aesthetically beautiful and reconfirms my thoughts above.

RATING: 6.5
I [b]Samurai[/b] kæmper man om indflydelse over tre forskellige typer af indbyggere symboliseret ved hjelme, buddhaer og rismarker. For at være med i opløbet om sejren forudsættes det, at man ved spillets afslutning har majoritet indenfor en af tre typer af indbyggere. Ens score afgøres dog af alle de indbyggere man har fået kontrol over med undtagelse af dem i den kategori, man har majoritet i. Så man bliver altså på den ene siden nødt til at specialisere sig for at være med i opløbet om sejren men på den anden side afgøres ens point af at man ikke har specialiseret sig for meget.

[b]Samurai[/b] er et elegant spil, der takket være en spilleplade med variabel størrelse skalerer fint mellem to og fire spillere. Det er i bund og grund en brainburner af den bedste eller værste skuffe alt efter smag og behag. Umiddelbart syntes spillet ikke at være blevet bedre med årene... måske er konkurrencen fra de mange nyere spil bare for hård. I hvert tilfælde forekommer spillet mig i dag at være for abstrakt brainburner-agtigt med to spillere. Med tre eller flere er det ikke mindre brainburner-agtigt, men her frygter jeg, at det kan gå helt galt, da der mellem ens ture kan ske alt for mange ting, man ikke kan kontrollere. Spillet har i hvert tilfælde fået opdateret sin score på BGG for mit vedkommende og er p.t. nedskrevet med 1½ point. Forhåbentlig kan det med tiden genvinde lidt terræn, men jeg er ikke sikker på, at det vil lykkedes for det.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
 Now. The most beautiful game I own. Plexiglass bits are intriguing and full of elegance. The idea of puzzle-like connecting board parts is ingenious. The board itself is as elegant as the bits. All together feels a bit taoistic and zen-like. 

Samurai is a pure abstract strategy or rather tactic game. But it's simple and really brilliant. 

There is a nice learning curve for this one. 
First you just learn what does the tokens do, when and where to use them. You start to create your own ideal starting combination and find that the placement of figures is the little pregame in the game.
Then you find that using some combinations of tokens could change the board situation drastically. You start to analyze what tokens are still in the stack waiting to be revealed - here comes the ,,probability" aspect of the game.
Great!

It's a very tense, but a little bit more chaotic game for 4. Need to check with 3.
===
Edit: After second game with 4 rating rised to 9.2. You have to learn how to play with more players, cause new tactics and strategies have to be used.
[20-25 minutes for 2p]

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
[b]Samurai[/b] [i]Reiner Knizia 1998[/i]
Samurai is a tile placing game with really easy rules and room for a lot of tactical situations in which players must use their skills to get the victory. The scoring system is a typical Knizias one what means that the end game will depare a lot of surprises and though decisions. Its scalability is very good and the replayability is good. The board is a map of Japan that can be build using different configurations according to the number of players. Although you could think at first sight that is an easy game it has actually a lot of deep and rewards good and wise playing, and this is very good because its rules are really easy to grasp, almost the same level of difficulty of some fillers. A gem that worths its inclusion in any collection and is a very good choice for beginners and casual gamers.

RATING: 4.5
A game where everything you work for can be for nothing, depending on who gangs up on you (sometimes through incompetence).  The game often ends suddenly.  The flair of the game is being able to suddenly change the direction of the game abruptly, turning the strategy focus from one goal to another.  You don't know who is actually winning, unless there is a runaway victory over newbies.  Normally if you win, it's accidental or everyone else messed up to give you the victory.  The ability to suddenly end the game often suddenly appears, and the sense to forgo this honour is only rarely achieved, despite it normally throwing the game to someone else.  This is quite a complex game and if thought through properly should take quite a long time.  Instead it is treated as a parlour game, which it is if you don't care that you just let someone else win through a mindless move.  Personally I don't really care for Samura all that much.  It's just too easy to get run over by the other players who inadvertantly conspire against you in several of the small battles.

RATING: 5
Gameplay rates a 4, but the visual presentation is an upper-deck homerun 10, which bumps my rating to 5.  Samurai fails to excite me because it feels very static.  I can't really do anything very interesting on the board, I'm mainly just trying to keep my options open and flexible, set up some future opportunities, and avoid handing the golden goose to my left-hand neighbor.  None of that interests me much.  It feels sort of like I'm playing the pencil and paper game Dots and Boxes, in which the player who closes out a box wins the point.  In Samurai, the player who places the final tile and initiates scoring has so much power that the whole affair becomes a waiting game.  Everyone goes around the table making safe moves, waiting for an opponent to open the door for them to make their move.

Then there's the scoring. Knizia is without question the master of creating balanced, complex, and unstraightforward scoring mechanisms.  It's a tool he uses to enrich gameplay by making it tough for the players to pinpoint the value of an action, a tile, a card, a token, a point, a buck, a gizmo, or whatever.  But I think he overreached in Samurai; the arcane victory conditions screw up the game because they're just too opaque.  It's not really possible to form a heuristic for good play without memorizing the other players' figure counts.  If your mental count is off by just one figure, your carefully-planned winning play may actually forfeit the victory to an opponent.  For this reason, I recommend playing with the captured figures in front of the player screens.  The scoring system is still too unstable, but it will help enormously those who want to play intelligently without having to memorize as many as nine separate running totals.

A game that masquerades as a perfect information game with a variable set-up but has just enough variability through tile draw to not make the outcome discernible before the game begins (as can be the case with pure perfect information games when players are not on even playing fields, for diverse reasons). Simple rules, elegant play, and creative scoring make this game easy to get into. However, as the good doctor always does, easy to learn does not always mean easy to master. While the interaction might not be as rich as Tigris and Euphrates, the choke points are more obvious - which can make even a first play contentious! Not to mention how aesthetically pleasant and functional the board and the bakelite pieces are. 

Here's hoping for a FFG reprint in their new Euro Classics line.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
[Initial Impression: 8]
Samurai is a truly excellent area-influence game that scales well from 2 to 4 players.  The scoring system feels a bit odd at first, but once you've learned it, the system seems natural and sets up some interesting strategies (i.e., don't devote more effort to collecting a given type of figure once you have a plurality).  The bits are great (especially those little buddhas!), and the variety of tiles provides plenty of opportunity for strategic planning and for tense tactical decisions. I find the two-player game to be much more thought-provoking, tense, and competitive than with higher player counts, probably because I can more easily track the current score and try to plan out the best possible move.  One drawback is the randomness of the tile draw, which can limit your options at critical points - it might be interesting to try a variant where each player has all tiles available from the start (you might also need to impose a limit of one special character tile per turn), although this could slow the game down considerably.

RATING: 5
It feels too much like chess, especially with only two people. Some plays seem fairly deterministic. With three or four, it is slightly more interesting.

Update (02/24/11): Finally was "in the mood" after two years, but quickly recalled why I am reluctant to bring this to the table. If playing against capable opponents, it seems that most of the game is spent setting up for big plays, like winding up a clock... with pressure building up slowly and then bam! The last third of the game quickly resolves itself in a cascade-like fashion of falling dominos or a deflating balloon. Another player commented that, yes, it is mostly deterministic, although the hidden nature of tiles makes it interesting. With three or four players, suboptimal plays and mistakes from inexperience can be quickly magnified into huge swings for an experienced player. It is a great abstract, with a thinly pasted on theme, but just seems out of place with the other board games I have. It plays much heavier, if one takes this seriously, than its light ruleset suggests. It's a solid '5'.

Interesting and recommended variant: randomly place all scoring figures, and have all players randomly draw their first five tiles.

RATING: 8
First played March 26th, 2007.  This is a quick learn (~5min) and quick play (~45min for 4).  It has a good mixture of luck and strategy.  Your objective is to capture the most of one or two types of figures (Budda, Rice, High Hats)  Getting two you win.  If you get one, you are in the running.  Of those left, whoever has the most pieces of the figures they didn't have the most of, wins.  You capture the figures by placing tiles next to cities (gaining influence).  Once there are no land spaces left, the pieces are evaluated for ownership.  Figures go out of the game for ties.  You can play "quick" tiles to play more than one tile during your turn.  Each player normally plays one tile.  I'd strongly recommend giving this a try if you know someone who has it.  Give it consideration for purchase and for a spot in your rotation of regular game plays.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Ideal Players: 2-4

Description: Vie for influence in feudal Japan in this simple tile-laying game. Players must have the most of one type of influence to be eligible to win, but are then scored on all other types.

Pro: Beautiful components. Chess-like strategy. Limited hand of tiles forces interesting choices. All players have an equal chance of winning (use the same tiles). Hidden information creates tension. Plays quickly.

Con: A little abstract.

RATING: 8
After 8 plays on the computer this is a well thought out brilliant little abstract strategy with tile laying, set collection and area influence aspects. I think this game defines what people like to refer to in games as "elegant". This rates right up there with chess and go IMO, doesn't have anywhere near the depth but it scores points in other areas like 2-4 players, plays quickly with very little AP and it's just cool and quite fun. Neat scoring system and a "screw you" tile thrown in there for good measure. Awesome for 3 which I suspect is the sweet spot, not sure with 4 yet but 2 is still a very good little tactical battle. Some luck with the tiles but that keeps it interesting I think, variants could probably overcome this somewhat as well. If you like abstracts you should try the trial like I am, I'm hooked now!

[u]UPDATE[/u]:After 10 more plays and buying the board game it now feels like there isn't as much to explore anymore. Beautiful components and great little tactical game thats fun for the weeknights but lacking depth in the strategy department, also works as a gateway game, best with 3 but 2 and 4 great as well and still a weeknight favourite. Rating down slightly now from 8.75 => 8

RATING: 7
We bought this and have played several times with 2 players, and it works well.
--------------------------------
We've only had one multiplayer session. Two first timers, two with more experience. We played with a random setup, which would change once we've played enough to get more subtle. The play flow had a really balanced and elegant feel of play vs counterplay, measured decisions vs mixed opposition, looking to create or find or block points of leverage. There were clearly better strategical choices to be made than some of the ones I picked along the way, but the tactical play was engaging from the word go. For my mind the game presentation meshes extremely well with the mood of the play. After one play, I want to buy this. Looks like it would scale well. The scoring outcome felt slightly chaotic.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Knizia tile-laying game where you play tiles around cities in Japan in order to claim token(s) placed on that city at the beginning of the game. There are three different kinds of tokens. The tiles range from 1-4 in value and have pictures of specific token or samurais (which are essentially wild and can influence any token). Once a city is surrounded, the player with the highest total value for that kind of token wins it. The player who collects the most tokens out of any type usually wins (or you have the most in two types, or you have the most total tokens, etc.). 

The game almost qualifies as an abstract in its simpicity. It's a clever game that rewards clever play, but it's not a deep or rich as Knizia's great tile-laying classic, Tigris and Euphrates. Once the cities around a token are claimed, the played tiles cease to have any meaning (other than to remind players what tiles are left). I think in great tile games (like T&E and Acquire) played tiles continue to influence play until the game's end. Even still, this is enjoyable and the production is gorgeous. 


=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

[3-players] Fun game with similarities to a game of chicken (who's going to put the other in a position to capture a city?). Very enjoyable with the possibility of playing very quick, too (though AP could be a challenge for some). Also, really awesome bits!

RATING: 7

[color=green]
LAST PLAY: 2014
[/color][color=blue]
Good game.

It doesn't matter you play it with 2, 3 or 4 people, this game holds everything. I'm really impressed by how Knizia could create a game like this only using 4 numbers and a few tiles.

If players are both similar ranked, games are very competitive and it's very replayable.

I prefer to play it in a 3 player version, but for two people it's also very good. For 4 people the game goes on perfectly, but it's a little bit more difficult to have the control of the game I guess.

(Dec. 2010) After a lot of plays I'm thinking like other players: it's a kind of Tic-Tac-Toe. It is a good game but NOT a great game.

RATING: 7.5
A tense, tile laying game.  Much of the game's outcome can be determined by each players initial selection of five tiles at the beginning of the game.  Chose wisely.  Three players is definitely the sweet spot for this game.  I am not sure, but I sense there may be a first player advantage in the two-player game.  In a four player game, there is a chance of (accidental) king making, especially if one of the players is not at the same skill level as the other players.  At three players, this game really shines.  This game is both one of my favorite tile-laying games and one of my favorite Knizia games.

RATING: 7
Samurai is a game of majorities. In a 4-player game, 39 majority battles to get one of the tokens placed randomly on the board at start are fought simultaneously. Each of these tokens belongs to one of three factions, with the ultimate goal for the players to achieve a majority in one of the three factions. Only players with a majority are considered for the win unless no player was able to gain a majority. To earn such a token, players place tiles on the board influencing either a specific faction or all of them but then to a lesser degree. A few special tiles allowing to swap tokens, re-use tiles or placing more than one tile at once spice up the game.

Samurai is an old-fashioned Euro, one of the classic Knizia’s. It’s confrontational in a way that’s unusual for modern Euros. It has a strong right-player binding. Unthoughtful moves by the previous player leave chances for the following player and can easily decide the game. Due to only three different factions, Samurai is probably best at that count. With four players, two players likely will have a harder time than the others, battling it out for the majority of the same type. A good game but potentially only great with experienced players of similar skill at the right player count. Under these conditions, a whole new subtle game of incentivizing other players to do mutually beneficial moves could arise.

RATING: 8.5
A lot of "Take that" confrontation, without the need to get upset because there are some many other opportunities on the board. 4-player feels a little chaotic to me must because the board is so long and I couldn't focus on the other side and a lot can happen on the other 3 players turns that you can't take advantage of.Typical Knizia scoring system of attaining multiple types of sets (Religion, Military, Economy), but forcing you not to focus on one.  This is my favorite implementation because you compare who has majorities (if you don't have a majority on any of the 3 sets, you are automatically out of the winner's circle) and then compare minorities for the winner.You can choose any five of your tiles to start, but from there drawing is random.

Pros: Tactical Tile Laying, beautiful components, 3 piece board that adjusts to 2-4 players, Plays fast
Cons: Random tile draw leaves you shafted at times, Pasted-on (albeit aesthetically pleasing) theme

RATING: 8
Samurai is a great balance between deep strategy and luck.  Having a hand of tiles to manage mitigated the randomness of the draw, but still limits choices enough to prevent much analysis paralysis.  Playing well still requires a grand strategy on what to pursue, but you need to play well tactically as well to execute your plan and to prevent setting other players up.  It's fast, tight, and entertaining - one of Knizia's best!

RATING: 8
[b]Purchase/Selling History[/b]: 
First played on computer, then at Barringer's house at christmas party '06.  Bought on ebay fall '07.
Sold to Jason Nov '08

[b]Rating: 8.0[/b]
[i]Design rating[/i]: [b]4[/b]
[i]Component rating[/i]: [b]1[/b]
[i]Willingness to play rating[/i]: [b]1.5[/b]
[i]Fun rating[/i]: [b]1.5[/b]

[b]Players considerations[/b]:

RATING: 8.5
The game is rather light but very engaging. It plays smoothly. Once learned it's super easy to play. The quality of components is great, the game is stylish and beautiful. 
The only problem is it's description. It's not a war game or historical game- it's simply a logic game with some japanese theme in the artwork - it could be just numbers and colours instead and the map could be replaced by squared or hexed paper. Explaining the core of the game takes about 15 seconds. So don't expect anything epic. 
It takes from half to one hour but requires lots of concentration and thinking, so with good opponents that is enough. 
It reminds me of Neuroshima Hex pretty much but I like it better- especially for 3 or 4 players. It's much more accessible and universal theme-wise and again, it looks gorgeous

RATING: 6
I actually like the game better than you may suppose based on my rating. It's a terrific puzzle game with a domino effect that plays out magnificently. The rating should be an 8 but I have a pet peeve with games that pretend to be a theme but are truly abstract puzzle games. Do not buy this game thinking you're going to be getting a game about Samurai or Japan. The mechanics would have worked just as well with colored blocks and shapes. Slapping a theme onto a mechanic makes me feel as though someone in the marketing department had too much to say. Okay, my beef aside, this is one of the few abstract/puzzle games I recommend and feel most gamers should have in their collection.

RATING: 8
Samurai looks brilliant: the gameboard is beautiful (althought it is a little thin) and gamepieces, those little buddhas, ricefields and high-helmets, are awesome. The Japan-theme is there, you can almost feel it. 

The game mechanic is simple (tile-laying) but it offers a lot of tough decisions. As usual, Knizia has made very interesting scoring system: because every player keeps their scores hidden (and 'who is the winner' -system is quite complicated) the excitement lasts all the way to the end.

This is definitely a classic!

RATING: 8
I had been craving [thing=3][/thing] for some time and was happy to receive it in a trade.  The mechanics and theme just felt very clean and elegant to me, and the components looked great.  I had played the computer version previously and I was particularly hopeful that this would be a good two player game for us, as we've enjoyed other abstract Knizia titles like [thing=50][/thing], [thing=760][/thing], and [thing=9446][/thing]---games where you fight for a target by laying down cards of specific values.  [thing=3][/thing] adds a spatial component as well as interesting scoring system to the mix.  

Unfortunately, this is one of the games where our tastes diverge coompletely---she couldn't really stand it.  I was a little surprised since it felt somewhat similar to the cardgames listed above.  Mostly, she really doesn't like spatial games like this.  Despite the nice components, the gameplay feels very dry and abstract.  Plus, especialy for two, the zero-sum gameplay feels very relentless and cutthroat and, in some cases, mean.  For example, using the special token to switch out a token to mess up her carefully laid plans felt more than a little mean to her.

Sadly, this probably won't be getting that much playtime, although I do feel like this would be a very good two player game for the right set of people.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Based on two-player games.  Once you have opened the box (do not get me started on the cover which looks like a poster for a terrible am-dram version of a Gilbert & Sullivan opera - a more stylised artwork I feel would be more suitable) it is wonderful.  The pieces celebrate the aesthetic traditions of Japan (specifically their lacquerware), the rulebook is clear and comprehensive, the game is highly accessible*, and the game allows a level of strategy and tactics which is not exclusive to the experienced gamer.  Samurai plays quickly, though with each turn there are several choices to weigh-up, and certainly leaves you wanting to play more.

Though at its heart Samurai is an abstract game the attention to detail enthuses the game with theme and atmosphere which is often lacking in some games who have grand thematic pretensions.  In 2007 the British Museum hosted an exhibition entitled Crafting Beauty in Modern Japan and I feel that Samurai would sit comfortably among the exhibits (if you remove the cover).  Knizia is undoubtably a 'national living treasure.'

*Gameplay is colour dependent therefore players who suffer from levels of colour blindness will find difficulty playing the game
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
This is a fine game.  Firstly, the presentation (however fake the characters) is well done and the tactile feel of the pieces has much to recommend it, even if the game does not really reflect any connection to the theme other than the shape of the islands).  Then game itself is a pleasing mix of tactics and strategy - you need an overall approach but you have to react to the evolving board.  There is some luck in the draw of the tiles (reduced if you pick your opening 5 tiles) and the end of the game can sneak up on you - more than once neither player has realized the last token of one type is gone.  Determining the winner can feel a bit odd too, with a list of conditions and results, but overall it is a winner.

RATING: 7
A classic Reiner Knizia game where you are placing tiles to surround cities and towns.  Once completely surrounded, players with most Buddhas, Rice or Noble Hats, collect the tokens.  You are trying to get the most types of goods in each category.  Players compare the lowest number of items collected and determine who is lowest and that player is eliminated.  You continue to compare types of goods and determine whoever has the most wins.  Very simple to play but with many subtleties which make the game interesting.

RATING: 7.5
I love the black plexiglass pieces that come with this game. They feel great to play with, and look really cool on the game board.

There is a thin veil of theme here providing some context for what is essentially an abstract game. I wouldn't call the theme pasted-on; Knizia has very clearly developed the game with a Japanese theme in mind, it's just as a game it feels more like I'm placing tiles than conquering feudal Japan.

However, this is a very elegant game with some tough decisions and deep strategy, and a reasonable play time. I think it's a keeper.

RATING: 8
First impression after one 2p game and one 4p game.  I enjoyed this a lot more with 4p.  It starts slow, but builds in interest through the middle and end part of the game.  At the beginning of the match I was ready to put this in the forgotten pile of one time experiences, and by the end of the match I wanted to play it again right away.  Simple rules, short without being a filler, deep strategies, interesting board.  JATE (Just another themeless Euro) -- the theme is window dressing, this is an abstract game using numbers.  Having said that, the  FFG edition looks charming and is very enjoyable to see.  Maybe it's the area control aspect, maybe the fluid board, maybe the limited information and light touch of luck, but I rank this higher than I usually rank abstract games with no theme; Bravo Dr. Knizia.

RATING: 9
I love this game. It's easy to teach and people quickly catch on to all the strategies available to you on a given turn. There is a little bit of randomness to the game as you have to draw your replacement tiles but I think this really forces you to adapt your initial strategy to work with what your new tiles are each turn. 

Explaining the scoring to new people is a bit of a chore as it's so unusual, but after the first game, most people have it down. Don't get to play it enough.

RATING: 9.5
Genius! My gaming heaven! This is my favourite game, no doubt. And I believe this game is the definition of elegance. Simple rules, very fluid gameplay. But my brain always hurts when I play Samurai!

Samurai has a perfect game/time ratio. You don´t waste your time with paperwork. You simply play. You are thinking all the time.  

The game scales so well. It´s excellent with 2, 3 or 4 players. 

On top of all this, you will find a very clever scoring system, based on majorities. This is the best part. You don´t play to collect the most pieces. You play to have 2 majorities. Brilliant!

Some may say it´s a very mathy game. Yes, it is. But it´s a matter of taste. And some may say the game ties too much. Well, I think it´s an important part of the game, you need to know the tie breakers to optimize your moves, but I don´t see it as a failure of the game.

RATING: 9
This may turn out to be my favorite Knizia. It's absolutely brilliant.

I thought the theme was pasted on, but it isn't. A few turns in, I started feeling like I was watching feudal Japanese politics play out -- and this being a Knizia game, the beautiful math and efficiency underlying everything made it incredibly tense. Waaay more tense than I expected.

In my first game, I felt like I was floundering early on, then saw that I had a chance to pivot midgame, and then closed out by realizing just how much I'd shot myself in the foot with my early plays. One particularly boneheaded play could have dramatically improved my final position, though not far enough to win. Every move is important, and everything speeds up as the board starts to fill up -- and suddenly it becomes difficult to play without also improving someone else's lot.

The dash of chance (tile draws) is just right. I love that everyone chooses their opening hand, and then seeds the board based on their (nascent, secret) agenda; I love it just as much how much of the game thereafter feels like it's played on shifting sands.

I can see a ton of depth lurking under the surface, too -- and a metagame that would develop based on repeated plays with the same folks. There's an entire of stuff to think about underlying what is, on its surface, a fairly simple game.

I adore turn angst, and the dial on this one goes to 11. It's an incredible game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This is a fun and tense game where you are doing your best to win multiple "battles" (majorities) across a map - in Knizian fashion the scoring is the name of the game, you need a majority in one of the three types, but beyond that you need the most of the ones that aren't your majority. Your tiles are drawn at random and you always have a hand of five, however - as the game draws further you can predict your and your opponents draws rather well which means that it doesn't feel "lucky", they simply force you to react differently and plan differently (in terms of managing your tempo and how you spread out).

The pace is rapid, much more so than Tigris & Euphrates, though this game is no less "thinky" per se. You must consider the spatial aspect, but also your influence "count" everywhere - knowing when and where you can lose is just as important as knowing where to push for victory!

I really want to try this more, and while it might not reach the heights of Tigris for me it won't be more than a step behind - also, they don't compete that much as this one does 2-player better than Tigris in my opinion.

A great game!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Game in a Nutshell: A light tile-laying, majority game where players try to secure the right amount of three different kinds of figures.

Played as: 2,3,4 player

Pros: It has that perfect blend I love of personal hand (tile) management intersecting with reacting to the current situation of the game as decided by your opponents. Especially nicely done here is that everyone should have all the same tiles available to them at one point or another.

Cons: It's probably a little too dry (or abstract) to excite casual gamers. The conflict is really only coincidental (basically forced by the game), non-aggressive, and probably predictable over time. I think that diminishes the tension of the player interaction to some extent. Scoring was a little confusing. Four player games while recommended, can be a little crowded.

Other: The game's play time is equally ratio-ed to the number of players playing. Probably 5-10 minutes per player depended on how analytical they tend to be. 

Best Use: A lower conflict, easy to learn tile laying game for people who don't need to be convinced games are fun.

RATING: 7
Played once.  An area-influence game where one attempts to collect pieces (towers, buddhas, or cubes) by placing influcence markers.  The scoring is unique, in that to win the game you _must_ have a clear majority of at least one type of piece, but then your points are equal to the number of the total number of _other_ types of pieces that you have.  Very cool. 

I _love_ the thoughtfulness of this game; the game requires a tremendous amount of tactical thinking before placing each tile, especially as the game progresses and the board is filled with a mishmash of tiles.  Since you know exactly how many influence tiles each player has, it quickly becomes possible to consider everyone's possible move, and thus determine which of yours is ideal.  One must chew over each tile placement, and the flavor builds with time.  

The major problem with the game (and the reason it doesn't have an 8, 9, or 10 ranking from me) is that the number of pieces each player has collected of each type is hidden from the other players.  Given that this information is critical to victory in the game, this means that to play well you _must_ memorize the number of pieces each player has collected (since collecting too many of any one type of piece would hurt your score).  Yet memorizing the pieces everyone has collected is tedious and annoying, and detracts from the fun of the game.  Playing with the pieces open removes nothing from the game, yet gives the skilled tactician everything they need to know to make the best moves, without giving an unfair advantage to those who are willing to memorize information.

RATING: 7
In Samurai, players use a stylized map of Japan as the game board. The goal is to collect an assortment of different pieces (buddahs, high hats and rice fields). The game board is well made and conceived. Likewise the pieces are nicely done (probably the best part of this game).  Players take turns and place tokens (tiles) on the board to surround these pieces and capture them. As typical with Reiner Znizia%u2019s games, the game mechanic is simple and easy to grasp but there is lots of strategy involved in placing tokens. Also typical of Znizia, the game is somewhat abstract and the most complicated aspect is scoring at the end of the game. Player interactions come in the form of out maneuvering other players in placing and capturing the pieces you need.

I did enjoy Samurai and liked its simple and Go like game play of placement and control. I recommend it if you%u2019re looking for something slightly less abstract than Go or wanting to play a Go game with more than two.

RATING: 6
I definitely appreciate Samurai's design. As usual with any quality Knizia game, the rules are simple, but the decisions are interesting. Despite the straightforward rules, it took me a while to "get" the game, about a dozen plays to pull off a couple wins in a row. As expected, the complexity is between Tigris & Euphrates and Through the Desert, but the game surprisingly takes about the same amount of time as the latter, maybe 15 to 20 minutes per player.

Unfortunately, apart from its superior components, Samurai doesn't seem to have any advantages over its counterparts for face-to-face play. It lacks the drama or depth of Tigris & Euphrates but isn't as accessible as Through the Desert. (See charlesf's and chally's comments for some astute observations in this vein.) On the iPad, however, where Through the Desert is thoroughly played out and Tigris & Euphrates totally lacks the compressed epic feel it has face-to-face, Samurai is a nice diversion.
