=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Medieval Japan is at your feet. Your task is to acquire the support of the three factions in the country - military, priests and peasants - and you do it by placing your forces around the cities and villages. When surrounded, the faction joins the strongest force of their kind. That's about it!

What then is so great about Samurai? 

Firstly it is the simplicity. The game is quickly learnt and quickly played. 

Secondly it is the elegance. The pieces are exquisite and the map size changes with the number of players.

Thirdly it is the surprising depth. Your forces differ slightly (the strength rangens from 1 to 4 and some "wild" cards impact all three factions) and you need to place them carefully to optimize their total influence. 

Fourthly it is the fierce struggle hidden beneath the calm surface. Every tile placed is a fight for influence and you must always take into account your opponents' responses in order not to be beaten by the final tile surrounding a city or village.

Samurai does indeed stand a comparison with the classic go but with more players. Granted, this is not a game for everybody but if you do enjoy a chaotic brain-burner, there are few better games than this.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
My 14 year old nephew and I sat down to learn this together.  I had gone over the rules several times before and knew that the scoring was complex, but left it alone until we needed it.  It turns out, we did not even need it.  He soundly beat me the first game.  The game was easier to play than the rules were to understand.  The fun thing was, that after showing him several other games over the course of a week, it was his favorite.  I agree.  Fun theme, some strategy, tactics galore, confrontation everywhere.  When I lost, I still felt like I had still won some battles, and with a little better strategy, could have won the war.  We played our first few games by selecting random tiles.  The last game, we picked our tiles up front.  There is a reason why this game has been around so long and remains in the top 100 - it is really good.

Why this is not a 10:
-> I cannot get anyone to play with me...
-> Seriously, I prefer direct confrontation just a wee bit more so I would not want to play this all the time.

RATING: 10

RATING: 8
Meccaniche semplici ma strategicamente interessante. Gioco decisamente astratto (è di Knizia), solo grafica e titolo si rifanno al mondo orientale, ma il gioco è talmente carino che ce ne si dimentica. Scala benissimo al variare del numero dei giocatori. In due è un gioco quasi scacchistico, mentre in 3 e 4 c'è il rischio che un giocatore meno abile influenzi in maniera involontaria l'esito della partita. Gioco di piazzamento e, anche se l'interazione è notevole, il calcolo la fa da padrone. Esiste anche una componente 'fortuna' dovuta dalla pesca delle carte ma solitamente non determinante. Per meccaniche di maggioranza mi ricorda altri astratti quali Clans e Terra Nova, ma trovo Samurai superiore anche per l'idea del calcolo finale dei punti vittoria che ricorda giochi come Tigris & Euphrates e Himalaya.

RATING: 8
Typically sterile and cultured area control from Knizia. This might just be the best of his tile-laying series, thanks to a two-layered control strategy: a large scale one in the opening setup followed by the nitty-gritty of individual buildings. The oddball scoring makes a midgame switch of strategy both feasible and viable.

RATING: 9
Another Knizia game with simple rules in which the depth is revealed over time.  As in Tigris and Euphrates, a prevalent theme in the underlying strategy of this game is achieving a sense of balance.  The game is quick, especially with 2 players, but every turn matters.

UPDATE:  Ive played the ios version of this quite a bit lately.  I find it is MUCH easier to win when you go first in a 2p game.  If two players are evenly matched, it seems player 1 wins a majority of the time.  I've read in the forums where statistics have validated this claim.  Many strategy games do have a player 1 advantage, but Samurai seems to suffer from it more than many.

UPDATE:  I have recently picked this game back up.  Very addictive game.  The first player advantage is defintely there for skilled players.  But leaving a boat or two out for player 1 seems to even it out.  This game is a classic.

RATING: 8
Was given the game as a gift, and have played it eight times in two days (which is a lot for me).  I think Samurai's incredible.  Extremely simple rules and mechanics coupled with brain burning decision making.  It's tense from beginning to end.  All plays so far have been 2-player.  I'm really looking forward to adding on the extra islands with some friends.  Initial rating: (a strong) 8

3/28/14 - 20 plays, rating still 8 - I was surprised to see 20 logged plays, that is quite a bit higher than average in my collection.  I think it's surprising because I feel like I'm still just scratching the surface when it comes to mastering this deceptively simple to play game.  Samurai is excellent at all player counts.  The games are wonderfully tense, you are torn between reacting to your opponents and pursuing your own agenda. With only two actions per turn, you always feel vulnerable.  Anyone who generalizes euro-games as multi-player-solitaire needs to play some Knizia designs; Samurai being a particularly excellent one.

RATING: 8.5
Great abstract game, although strategy may sometimes be obscure, and with more than 2 players it may be difficult to lay down even good tactics, but it's definitely a masterpiece. Rules are immediate, gameplay is elegant, game is quick and very beautiful looking. It is even very original: despite being a rather old game, nowadays, I don't remember many other games later on with similar mechanics. Watch out: many people here note that the scoring mechanism is very original: it has been actually copied by many other successive games. The mechanics of the game, instead, are still unique.

RATING: 6
I consider Samurai as Lite-E&T for several reasons, but especially the pressure of balancing your victory conditions, though in Samurai it is alittle convoluted. Despite being played in half the time and less rules I find more time and effort spent in E&T more enjoyable. For me I think this is due to Samurai's tiles being so numerical and specific, though they do provide a richer narrative. The order they are drawn can hamper your ideal plays, which I can accept, but in E&T there is a sense you can find a solution to unfortunate tile draws.For me this solving is fascinating spatially. I can sympathise that in Samurai there is no immediate reward of a victory point, your mentally executed build up can be frustratingly undermined i.e. by swap tile, with no gain. But the experience that bothers me most is there is an overpowering deduction element to the game in order to play well:what tiles are in your draw pool;what tiles could your opponent have; how many buddha's etc. All this can be replayed by number crunching the board as to who took what and if it wasn't for the swap tiles, hiding the vp's would be redundant. In E&T you often only need to deduce an opponent's lack of a colour.

RATING: 8
A simple but deep strategy game that plays quickly. The rules are easy to teach, but the complexities of the choices they present are immediately obvious. There's a lot to think about while playing. I like how even setting up the board is part of the strategy of the game, where all the players try to decide which resources they are going to go for and put them in exploitable configurations. The scoring is very interesting too, where you have to consider which resources your opponents are going for and how that affects your ability to get majorities and/or the most non-majority resources. The art and components of the Fantasy Flight version are very well done too.

RATING: 7
This is a game that I know from experience (namely Modern Art) is a game that I should like. It's got a complex 'mathy' tile laying mechanic (I don't even know how to describe the nature of it, 'geometric' doesn't seem right at all), and there's the standard Knizia touch of trying to get the most of things while getting as few as possible of what you have the most of. A sort of backwards optimization that reminds me of when I played Ingenious against computers and was savaged. 

I can sorta grasp that there's some value to using your pieces to grab things other people are spending lots of tiles towards, and at the same time trying to keep control of the board. Is this area control in a fancy sort of way? This is one of a few big tile-layers from Knizia, do they all sorta feel this way? I'm looking forward to playing it again, but I'm still not convinced if I like it or not..

RATING: 7
Dec 2016: Play with open scoring. Much better game.

Upgraded from 6.5 to 7. Good, but not as great as Tigris & Euphrates.

Very, very accessible and interactive game. Don't like the fact that once a scoring marker is captured, tiles around it are not that useful for the rest of the game, so choices get more limited as the game progresses.


May 2010: What I like:

* It's a tile laying game that allows you to play tiles anywhere on the board -- unlike most tile laying games in which you have to place a tile adjacent to one already on the board.

* It's almost like a trick-taking game in which each statue on the board represents a trick and the tiles played around it represents the trick itself. The nice thing about this one is that tile stay on the board, so you don't have to remember what you opponents have played already (unlike most trick-taking games).

What I don't like

* Considering how elegant the game play is, the end-game scoring is quite convoluted. Personally, I don't have much of a problem with it, but this is an actual barrier that prevents it from being a great gateway game. Also, the computational nature of the game (players have to constantly add things up to determine their standings for each statue), along with the end-game scoring, significantly puts a damper in the flow of the game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
I had so much fun playing this game. What started as quick game to play before the rest of my friends arrived, quickly became a highlight of that evening. I only meant to have a single game, and yet we ended up having 3 games, (that streak only interrupted by more guests arriving and expanding beyond the 4 player count). At the end of the session, one friend who had never played the game before, said "he might buy that game", which was huge as he owned no board games.

Samurai is relatively easy to teach. It has simple strategy of laying tiles to get an advantage influence advantage over your friends. A great puzzle that allows for smart plays. Could not recommend enough =)

RATING: 7
2-4 players, best with 3.  30-60 min.  Medium weight.
Abstract tile-laying game with just the right amount of combat and constant interaction. Interesting scoring rules. Punishes obstinacy -- you need know when to escape a failed strategy or you will lose. Help yourself without helping others; put yourself in a position where others must help you to help themselves; pit your opponents against one another; use ship tiles and samurai early or for a surprise move; and always keep track of the score (or better yet -- play without hidden captures).

RATING: 6
Samurai is a good game, but the victory conditions are needlessly confusing and just plain arbitrary--It is quite possible for the who captures the fewest pieces to win. 

Andy:  6B, 6H, 3R
Betty: 6B, 0H, 3R 
Carl:  0B, 6H, 3R
Diane: 0D, 0H, 4R

Diane wins because of the ties no wins either buddhas or hats, but if any of the first three players has one fewer buddha or hat, some other player wins.  Or Andy had captured one more rice, he'd win.

This fundamentally different from E&T/Ingenious where the "Highest low score wins" translates to a simple strategy of "always work on your lowest scoring color" and "AMAP score evenly in all colors"

RATING: 3
[color=#CC0000]

"Too clever by half."

First, in my opinion the theme is just goofy, and conflicts with the game that hides under the covers.  I can get past mercenaries being worried about defending rice fields, but aspects of the game when combined with the theme indicate the sudden relocation of buildings or even farm fields to locations hundreds of miles away.  It just doesn't make sense.  I know, it's a game, I shouldn't care.  But if you're gonna make a theme where you move something, farm fields, swampy ones in particular, are a bad choice.

The geometry of the board is very carefully designed to make all conflict more or less uniform.  Every single play-space on the board borders [i]exactly[/i] two prize-spaces.  So there's no hot-spots, no places where you need to get in early or in force in order compete.  Though the artwork is beautifully illustrated, the underlying game structure is a uniform layer of gray paint.  Nothing draws the eye of a seasoned gamer in this one, no place on the board attracts attention or breeds concern.  There's not one single piece of high ground on the entire map.  That's intentional.  This is an abstract bent to fit the shape of Japan.

Because no place on the board is particularly more valuable than another, fiefdoms are arbitrary.  While this would appear to be a good foundation for a game of intense mental competition, some of the playing pieces allow you to just bounce the pieces on the board at an arbitrary moment in time.  Any tactical advantage that you grind out through careful play disappears in an instant by your opponent airlifting a rice field or temple, or magically moving an already-played piece to a new location.  After careful planning and strategy, after good play, you find yourself holding an empty cup.

But hey!  Random elements are good!  I LIKE working with risk!  It keeps a game fresh!  You just need to plan contingencies.  But how do you do that in Samurai?  There's no significant way to play your options.  The nature of the game means that it's virtually impossible to plan for the contingencies of someone yanking terrain out and moving it.  In theory you could move it back with [i]your[/i] yank-it piece, but it's too arbitrary a factor to make a sound assessment of risk and reward.  There simply isn't sufficient footing.  So each piece played is played in the moment, no looking forward, no hedging your bets.  So it's pretty much flat.  You can keep pieces until the end, but everyone else is doing that too.

So in theory, while half of you is managing strategy and tactics in a brain-burner setup the other half is forced to come to terms with "it's a whimsical game of fun and chance."  The two collided for me personally.  Throw in a complete dearth of critical or pivotal regions on the board and what results is trying to manage your few nuclear-grade options for onesie and twosie results.  I found myself not taking the time to make solid tactical decisions because they could be washed away on a moment's notice and weren't terribly valuable to begin with.  The game didn't call me to focus as others do.  At that point I became disenfranchised, the emotional immersion ended and I started looking over my shoulder to see what was being played on the table behind me.

Standard Knizia scoring strategy, which in other games like Ingenious I like but here is counter to theme and kind of arbitrary.  In my game with four players it seemed to completely break down, though with three I can't help but think it HAS to be better.  With four players it's the equivalent of the last-place horse being shot out from under its rider on the back stretch while the others continue to the finish line.  Knizia loves to mess with endings and in some games it works, in this one it just seemed hokey.

No clue why this game rates as high as it does.  Given the choice play Ingenious.  Ingenious is tight and unified, and produces a real narrative during play with points of high drama on the board and a need to nurture weak spots.  

[/color]

RATING: 8
Surprisingly thematic for Knizia. As the boat and ronin tiles are played, you really get a feeling of a reinforcements swooping in (giving you the pros of a lucky game -- uncertainty and need to revise your strategy -- without compromising balance). 

The battle tends to move up and down the board, as the players contest the larger cities. The whole thing flows really well despite the strong seating influence (playtesting by Andreas Seyfarth, ha ha), with beautiful pacing and plenty of tactical choices. Scales very well from 2 to 3, but the 4P game is a bit too chaotic for my tastes.

Something that I love about this game is that the lengthy setup has a strong influence on the game's outcome. So it's 'game on' from the moment the box is opened.

Update: played it some more and it's still an 8 for me. The more that I play, the more subtlety is revealed. Recommended.

Updated - and a bit more in depth:
Medieval Japan - Samurai, buddhas, rice paddies and high hats.

Influencing the city and taking control - a complete shift in the thought process. I'll be the first to admit, it took me a little bit to develop a cohesive strategy; realizing that you need to win a few small skirmishes before you can win the big one. Do I commit all of my resources to this one city for a high number, or try and grab these three smaller ones instead. If I place this tile and win this city here, do I also give my opponent victories by completing the adjacent city?

I still don't think I've won, but again it is the playing of the game that is so enjoyable.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Got to try this on the iPhone against other players. Two marks of a well-designed majority game:

1. Simultaneously incentivizes the majority leader to “relax” instead of building the majority further, and the players playing catch-up to commit more resources (e.g. China, In the Shadow of the Emperor).
2. Allows you to control the timing of when majorities score (Stephensons Rocket, Tower of Babel)

Yes, that’s right: El Grande is a washed-up piece of FAIL using these two criteria.

Samurai is a clever design that passes both with flying colors. “Majority Relax” is prevalent in both the competition for individual figures and the end-game victory condition. The requirement to occupy a limited space, which is often shared between multiple arenas of competition, in order to add strength to an arena or two, makes for interesting spatial and timing considerations.

But I don’t love it as I’m not too enamored with the “each-person-has-own-fixed-draw-deck” concept (like Knizia’s Great Wall of China). It feels too much like a more random trick-taking (since you never know when you’ll get each tile), and I don’t like having to repeatedly keep track of everybody’s deck by looking at the board. I much prefer the concept in Knizia’s games where players draw from the same deck (Battle Line, Lost Cities), which allows you to bluff and push your luck.

Still, I think I’ll outgrow the own-draw-deck thing, especially since the rest of the game is good.

4+3

RATING: 8
I can see why this game fails to please people at the extremes. It doesn't offer the solid control or long-term strategy of Durch die Wuste, nor does it offer the simple fun of Einfach Genial. The scoring is kind of a bizarre perturbation of the mechanism used in Tigris and Euphrates. So what's going on in Samurai? Each player is trying to collect helmets, buddhas, and rice fields, and does so by surrounding these items with numbered influence tiles. You win if you manage to have the most tokens in at least two categories. But often, no one fulfills this condition. In a two player game, it is very common for each player to lead in one token type. To then determine who wins the tie, you check to see who has the most remaining tokens, once your leading tokens are removed. So, as in Ingenious and Tigris and Euphrates, it doesn't always pay to amass a huge number of only a single token. You need to strive for a balanced approach to win. You want to have the most of at least one kind of token, but shabby performance on other tokens will usually cost you the game. Thus Samurai rewards the player who can capture a broad variety of tokens, as well as take the lead in at least one category.

Given this kind of winning condition, the absense of total control in the game may frustrate some players. When you choose your moves, you have to plan for a rather wide variety of future contingencies - yet you must do this from a limited, random selection of your tiles. And the array of possibilities is too extensive to be subjected to thorough analysis during play. This isn't to say that analysis is impossible. Far from it! You must develop a sense of what can happen and act accordingly in order to win. Samurai doesn't offer total control for a few reasons: 1) you play tiles from a random draw (but you do get five tiles at a time to choose from) and 2) every player has one tile which allows them to exchange the positions of two selected tokens. So playing Samurai, you might become frustrated because you cannot execute a particular strategy for victory. I happen to like this about the game - that it forces you to play flexibly and adapt to ongoing developments. Any "strategy" you develop will only be good for about five turns - which is related to your five tile hand. As an aside, you do get to choose your initial five tiles. Despite this focus on tactical problem-solving, you still have to watch what is happening in the long run to do well. You don't want to allow opponents to lock out one of the three token types, for instance.

Samurai plays fairly quickly, scales really well from 2-4 players, and is a fun game. Some of my GeekBuddies note that it is missing the "fun factor", and although the gameplay is obviously "Knizia" and has that little bit of a dry feel to it, I disagree with them. I think Samurai is rather fun, and the best of Knizia's tile-laying trilogy. I am happy to admit that this is a matter of taste, however. One neat aspect of Samurai is that, especially in 3 and 4 player games, it boasts player elimination. This is why you keep the tokens you have won a secret behind your screen - if everyone displayed them, player A might realize well before the game is over that it is impossible for him to win. But given the tricksyness of the scoring conditions and player A's non-eidetic memory, it is doubtful player A will be able to figure this out before the game concludes. And like I said, it plays fast enough that it doesn't really matter. In the two player game, you do not hide your tokens. Occasionally, this means that two astute players will see who the inevitable winner is before the last tile is layed. In one recent game, the outcome had been decided when there were still seven tokens (one third of the total) yet to be claimed. This is a minor gripe, if it is a gripe at all. The point is: the game does reward skilfull play and does punish poor play - enough to occasionally eliminate players.

If you like games that have some strategy, but more of a tactical focus, and you don't mind a little bit of randomness and loss of control, give Samurai a try. I think it's a lot of fun!
(I only have one concern - not my idea originally - that Samurai may fall prey to Tic-Tac-Toe-ism, that is, that it is "solvable" and that for two players who have solved it the outcome will be (90%) a matter of turn order.)

RATING: 6
I've dropped my rating on this, as recent plays have left me a bit cold.  It's taken me some reflection to see what was bugging me.  The basic mechanic is similar to the old pencil-and-paper game 'dots and boxes', where the person who closes the box scores, and so the key defensive play is to NOT make the third side.
The problem is that Samurai is multi-player, so when you have one player who is careless/unskilled, the player immediately following reaps an undeserved and unbalancing bounty.  I don't care for games where you can win by choosing the correct chair at game start, and while I am exaggerating how big of a problem it has been so far, I can't deny that it has robbed Samurai of its early luster with me.  Off to the attic with you (for the time being)!

RATING: 8
Samurai by Reiner Knizia is an area influence game using tile-laying on a hex-grid of feudal Japan.  All players have identical hands of tiles.  The object is to collect majorities in captured pieces.  There are three types of pieces: Buddhas, Rice paddies, and High Helmets.  Equal numbers of each piece type are randomly placed in towns throughout Japan.  Pieces are captured by placing tiles around towns containing pieces.  When a town is surrounded by tiles, the pieces are given to the player(s) who had the most influence over them.  Tiles with a picture of a Buddha and the number 2, for example, exert an influence of 2 on the Buddha pieces in any adjacent town.  There are also wild card tiles (Samurai and Ronin for land, Ship tiles for sea hexes) that exert influence on all pieces in adjacent towns according to the number value of the tile.  Captured pieces are kept hidden, as the victory conditions depend on obtaining majorities.

Samurai has beautiful components, difficult decisions, ridiculously simple rules, and a satisfying conclusion.  2p and 3p are great.  I've only played one 4p game, which felt chaotic.

RATING: 5.5
I wasn't expecting a "samurai" game that came in a decent-sized box to end up feeling like a light filler game.  Certainly the theme is about as relevant as "Balloon Cup".  They could just as easily have made this game about space pirates gathering hazardous waste from Uranus, and it would not have lost any of its playability.  But that being said, I guess they had to choose SOME sort of a theme, so why not medieval Japan?  At least they have taken their chosen theme and created a very beautifully-produced game.  The problem is, it's just not that terribly interesting to play.  The four player game in particular seems a little too random for a game with so little randomness built into the mechanics, and leaves me with the odd feeling of having just one simple action to do but too many options for where to do it.  I'd much rather play a game of "Through The Desert" for a comparable level of simplicity and number of players.  Game design: 3.5/5  Personal enjoyment: 2/5  Total: 5.5/10
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
On a hex map in the shape of Japan, place cute plastic bits of three types (buddhas, hats, rice paddies) in the cities. Players place tiles valued 2-4 in those three types, plus wild tiles (samurai, boats) and special ability tiles (swap, move). When a city is completely surrounded, bits go to whoever has the highest strength in that suit.

A limited hand of 5 randomly-drawn tiles adds a bit of luck but keeps AP from being unbearable. Much of the game is in deliberately avoiding (or deliberately creating) opportunities for the next player, which creates an obvious left-right binding problem with more than two players of unequal skill. Very nice game, all up, with gorgeous production.

RATING: 7
[Hans im Glück 1998 edition] {Japan; tile-laying, area-majority, set-collection} Samurai is a nice tile-laying game with tenuous theme I would rather classify this as an abstract game even has it has partial information and involves tile draw. Scalable but it's too tight for a 2p game.

It's moderately deep and involves strong player interaction. A player has to continually evaluate the situation and react promptly to his opponents' moves, esp. the special ones. If he cannot predict how other acts, those special tokens can change the situation dramatically and destroy his entire game plan. Other than wisely playing the tokens, it is essential to carefully place figures and choose the initial 5 tokens during game preparation. One would have lost the game if he acted carelessly at the game preparation.

Luck is not important in this game although it involves tile draw. Players still have many controls over the tile draw by selecting the initial hand, having 5 tokens in the hand, determining the right time and place to place a token. I prefer to have some 1-2 samurais in my initial hand. The 'Ronin' token is of high strategic value - use it very wisely.

The unique, though strange, scoring system differentiates Samurai from other similar games. In Samurai, a player shall strive to dominate one figure and capture some other figures to secure his position. In addition, Samurai has a sudden game end. Therefore, players have to compete aggressively for the figures before the game suddenly ends.

The game components are sturdy and beautiful. The idea of assembling game board for different number of players is quite smart.

RATING: 7
Abstract tile-laying with a tenuous Japanese feudal theme. There are three different types of figures located on cities, towns and villages and the object is to surround these areas with tiles that correspond with the type of figure. There are 'wild-card' tokens such as the eponymous samurai as well as ones depicting ronin and ships, and once an area containing figures is surrounded the player with the highest total surrounding them wins the figure. It's a very simple game in many ways, especially to learn, but there is also a great deal of strategy required in order to play successfully. Like so many abstract games the theme feels like something of an afterthought and it could have been applied to any subject matter really. Nevertheless, this is a solid Reiner Knizia game but I feel it wouldn't be quite as highly regarded if it didn't have his name attached to it.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Played 10+ times.  Samurai is great.  Each player has an identical set of 20 tiles.  You have a hand of five and you are sending out your warriors to surround and conquer the various resources around Japan.  When a location is surrounded the one or more resources on the space are given to the player who committed the most effort towards winning that particular resource.  At the end if you have a majority in two of the three resources you win.  If not there is a complicated Reiner Knizia tie breaking mechanism. 

The pieces are beautiful and the game is quick and tense. I'm definitely a Samurai fan.  2008/12/15

RATING: 8
I am defintely impressed. A for concept, A for mechanics, etc. The design is very clean, I dont think we had any rules issues. The play is deep (we played 3 players)and the time frame is about perfect (45 min IIRC). Best of all it fills a hard to fill niche: an abstract game (arguably) for more than 2 players. To do this the game requires that you draw tiles to create some randomness which is a decent solution to the problem. 

HOwever, the issue comes down to whether the victory conditions are satisfying or not. With only 3 commodities and 3 players it is hard to win a convincing 2 commodity win (where one player gains a plurality in two commodities). Thus, each player wins one commodity and the winner is then the one who has the most of this non winning commodity. Ex:

A wins 3-3-2 commodities, B 4-2-2 C 4-3-1

Note you don’t  win the commodity if you tie, so it’s possible that B or C could have not won any. But assuming each player won one commodity, then A wins because he has 5 of the non winning brand (3+2). Was he clever or just fortunate in what the other two did? 

 It’s still a good game, but this aspect is troubling 
My feeling is that history will judge this game on whether the winning conditions are really clever or really just nonsense. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This game always challenges me and leaves me thinking what a simple yet brilliant game it is.  Every turn forces very difficult choices to be made.  The indirect scoring rules also provide an element of doubt to creep into your mind, which furthers your level of anxiety.  The game looks great, and the theme is good. RATING: 8
One of my favorite area control games. Very beautiful, simple, and deep. It's one of those peaceful, calming games; even when opponents foil your plans, it doesn't feel so much like a "block" as it does another piece of a puzzle you have to figure out. The components are gorgeous and fun to play with and the rules are extremely easy to learn. The game compels you to try many different strategies and seems like it would have a lot of longevity because of this. It scales very well from 2-4, as the map varies with different numbers of players. Sometimes, the game is decided long before it's over, but against equally matched players it can be tense right up until the finish. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 4
A wonderful, fairly abstract, but beautifully themed and cleverly designed game that suffers from three problems, two minor and one major.  The minor problems are: A) the endgame conditions, with open scoring, tend to produce a lot of AP and B) the random setup is awkward and the placed setup is tedious.

The major problem is that this game is, in my opinion, the poster child for horrible left-right binding.  The somewhat Go-like surround-to-score rule makes managing play tempo absolutely crucial and ensures that the winner *will* be the player to the left of the weakest player.

If you can ever manage to find 3 other players all equally strong, then this game is an 8; without this, it's a 2.  I've compromised and called it a 4.

RATING: 9.5
Tile-laying game with an oblique shogun-period Japan theme. Players have the same set of tiles from which 5 are selected to form their "starting hand" - the remainder will be randomly drawn to replace used tiles as the game progresses. Tiles are laid on a Japan-shaped board, to add influence on cities and eventually collect three different types of figures - Buddhas, Noble Hats, and Rice Fields. The winner will have the most figures in the most categories. Other than the less than intuitive victory conditions, Samurai is actually quite simple to learn but presents "gamer's game" depth. The modular board allows excellent scaling from 2P, 3P, and 4P. Serves a unique niche because I find it stands in the space between a gateway and a more complex gamer's game. Components are fantastic, especially the gorgeous Buddha, hat, and rice field figures.

It has some area control elements, and a Knizian majority scoring mechanism.  You place tokens trying to capture pieces. RATING: 8.5
A trivial-to-learn game with lots of tactical depth. Great with two players.

You have to capture pieces (warriors, peasants, rice) by placing more high-numbered tiles around them than your opponent.

I don't enjoy it as much as Through the Desert because it feels like a bit of a math-out. Lots of counting required to decide where to optimally place your tiles. 

The theme is also weaker than Through the Desert or Tigris and Euphrates. For a simpler game, I'd recommend Through the Desert. For a tactical civilisation/war game, I'd suggest Tigris.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A game we played on 4th August, and belatedly reported on, was the Knizia tile-laying Samurai-themed and self-titled; 'Samurai' which aims to recreated the strategic wars of succession during the medieval era of Japan's history. 

The aim of the game is to acquire (subvert/manipulate) the three key factions relating to the state; buke (nobility) represented by the castles playing piece, the religious castes represented by the shrines (Shintoism) and temples (Buddhism) and the heimen (farmers) represented by the wheat sheaf playing piece. They achieve this by using tiles representing influence amongst their clans who match the three playing pieces with matching pictures on tiles with an assigned range of values from one to four. The higher the value, the more effect they have, so placing them against the forty or so playing pieces is eventually resolved by comparing aggregate scores surrounding each playing piece. 

Each player draws four, if I remember correctly, specific tiles of their choice and hide these behind their nicely designed shoji screen, and depending on how many tiles are played during their turn (*), their replacements are the drawn randomly and turned over behind the player's screen, bringing their playing set back up to four. There are four or five tiles with special abilities to represent the machinations associated with siezing the shogunate and control of the emperor such as replacing a tile already played with this special tile so that the former's benefit may be re-used. The naval pieces are all special in that they can be played in addition to the usual one played by each player in their turn (*). Another tile allows a playing piece being potentially acquired to be swapped with another so that a concentration of buddha tiles suddenly are of no use when the playing piece is swapped with a wheat sheaf, for instance.

This skullduggery happened quite a lot during our game. The map onto which the playing pieces of the three factions are randomly placed in sequence by those participating represents the four main islands of Japan. The samurai tiles have one advantage, similar with the naval pieces too, in that they can be used to subvert any of the three types of playing piece, whereas the faction specific tiles can only be used against an identical playing piece. Therein lies the skill of placement. The blurb describes this quite succinctly in that players can make lightning-quick strikes with horseback Samurai and ships or approach their conquests more methodically. As each playing piece is surrounded, it is awarded to the player who has gained the most favor with the corresponding group, representing their successful subversion of that samurai clan or priestly or farming community. 

Gameplay continues until all the playing pieces of each faction have been removed from the board or four playing pieces have been removed from play due to a tie for influence. At the end of the game, players compare captured symbols of each faction type, competing for majorities in each of the three types. Ties are not uncommon and are broken based on the number of other, "non-majority" symbols each player has collected. When we played, the other three players did have a 'second go' which I think prejudiced the outcome as this enabled them to place more effectively which I did raise at the time. What's done is done but I am not an advocate of this given the rules were clearly explained at the start. In any event, I like the game, and it can probably be played in around forty-five to sixty minutes with new players and potentially, near to half that with those who have played a few times. A game i would recommend, Anjin-san.

RATING: 7
I love how the game has elements of slow accumulation of power and at the same time moments of "take that" thrown in, and it seems one has to balance the timing of one's power plays. Samurai has the advantage of being one of those beautiful, simple, yet engrossing games, which makes it perfectly suitable for introducing to non-gamers. On the other hand you have gamers who consider this their favorite game, and I can see why: it is a very elegant package of tension, some luck, and much to think about. An indispensable game in my collection, in large part because it scales from 2-4 players (although with 4 it can get a little chaotic).

RATING: 8
Hm.  There's almost a certain Age of Steam element to the game, in that the board is static, and the way that the pieces come out on the board dictates the strategy of your play. RATING: 7
An excellent game. Scales fairly well from 2-4 players. I am not too fond of it at 4 but would be willing to play.

The biggest thing going for this game is that it has a very simple ruleset (an important aspect for me in middle weight games) but fairly deep gameplay. So, it is easy to introduce it to new players and as they start playing it, they discover new layers to the game. 

 The game does give chances for some spectacular moves with the special tiles, which I like.  

The biggest negative is that it can get a little calculational towards the end and that can make it feel a bit dry/analytical

rating 8 with 2 or 3 players, 6 with 4

Set collecting.

Medium player interaction.
Very low theme application.

RATING: N/A
A tile-laying game is ostensibly set in medieval Japan. Players use hexagonal tiles to surround cities, which have one to three different figures: rice paddies, buddhas, or high hats. The tiles represent influence on particular facets of the cities, and the highest influence on a figure when the city is surrounded takes that figure. The object of the game is to have more of any one else in one category, and then have the most remaining figures.

Awards

1998 Meeples' Choice Award

RATING: 10
Set in medieval Japan, this is an  excellent tile-laying game by the German master designer Reiner Knizia.  Players compete to win over the peasant, noble, and religious classes of citizens in the various cities around Japan.  Whoever is most successful will be the new emporer.  It is fun, fast to setup & play, has nail-biting decisions and beautiful components.  The game is different with 2,3, or 4 players, but always fantastic.

RATING: 9
Brain-burner.  Tons of tactical decisions you must make every turn.  Not a theme that I normally have any interest in, but because it is from Reiner Knizia, it is an abstract.

I love this game!

My favorite Knizia game by a mile.  Because of the varied layout of the board and having no clue what tile you will draw next, this is a very tactically oriented game.  

It also has the best scalability of any game I've ever played.  Game is from two to four players, so for every player above two, all you do is add an extra piece to the game board. 

Area control mechanic.  Object of the game is to use your pieces to capture a number of Buddhas, rice fields and high hats (nobles or priests, I guess) which are inside of cities or villages.    To do this you use samurais, ships, and other pieces that vary in the amount of influence they project on the Buddhas, rice fields and high hats.

Very much a cat-and-mouse style of play, Samurai creates EXTREMELY HIGH tension.  If you don't fully think through on the benefits or consequences of laying a tile in a particular section of the board, you may be setting yourself up for a shellacking.

There are specialty tiles that allow you to move a piece you previously placed on the board as well as replacing a tile to your hand that was part of a captured territory. 

Fun.  Very tense, but a very enjoyable experience in gaming.  Simplistic rules with deep complexity levels.  

Needs to be in the Game Hall of Fame.  Wish there were one...  :)

RATING: 3
{ P - 2 [3] [4] }

Reasonably short and engrossing tile-layer with one inexorable problem. People place in good locations while avoiding set-up plays, but sooner or later the game arrives at the point where everything is a set-up play. Boats can help negate this, and there is a balance to be discovered between avoiding set-up plays and avoiding inefficiently capturing too many cities by surrounding them yourself, but every game I've played between people of equivalent skill boils down to end-game uncertainty. (Or worse, who didn't get screwed over on the draw.) For what it's worth, probably the coolest and most seamless method of player scaling.

RATING: 8
A tile laying game based on Japan, though it could easily have been designed with any other theme. Having said that, the use of Japan as a backdrop does add some flavour to the game.

The concept behind the game is relatively straightforward (maximising influence on neighbouring pieces so as to capture them). Randomness is introduced by the drawing of tiles. Players' strategy will have to evolve based on the tiles drawn. Use up your "good" tiles too early, and you will not be able to defend the latter pieces. Use them too late, and they will be ineffective! 

An intriguing game with some hard decisions to make.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
I believe Samurai by Reiner Knizia (Rio Grande) could very well be a model for "The Perfect Game". 

1) The rules are brief and easily grasped through the excellent examples, or after just one play 
2) The random tile and victory point distribution makes each game unique without over reliance on luck 
3) The classic ancient Japanese stylized gameboard and all the components are elegant and appealing 
4) The game is as engaging and challenging with 2 as it is with 3 or 4 
5) The scoring system used, and ability to shift tactics on the fly with the inclusion of specialty tiles, provides a player with several options on how to achieve victory.

RATING: 7

I know that it is supposed to be random (the drawing of the tiles) but I think it would be a better, yet diffent game if you were able to pick the tile you wanted.  In this reguard, I don't think it is a deep strategy game, but could be with the inclusion of this house rule.

RATING: 7
Super abstract, with a scoring system that's unusually dense even for Knizia.  Subject to AP and lots of downtime.

RATING: 8.5
The plexiglass pieces are the best bits I've ever experienced in a game.  The modular map of Japan, which merely omits an island for each player fewer, is simply brilliantly designed.  This is a fairly fast and sneaky game of influence and control.  Some people who play this seem to actively hate the scoring system's assertion that if two people tie for a lead, they are both worthless.  I love that mechanism:  It means you not only have to strive for a lead, but must be very attentive to attaining it.

RATING: 7.5
Very nice and easy game. Pros includes fast setup, simple rules (could be explained in 5-10 minutes) and short game play (less than hour). Game however allows some nice strategy and tactics and requires a bit thinking and planning to win. As cons (no everyone would agree those are cons) I could mention, that game requires little to no social interaction - it requires only logic and tactic skills, but no politics.

RATING: 7
Falls into the realms of Carcasonne which means its a winner with my wife and son. Reminds me of a mixture of Carcasonne and Lost Cities. Its not deep but its more confrontational than either of the former so its a nice little step up for the not so serious gamers.

Edit: I've played this a lot on iPad now and I can say that I don't like 4 player. Its impossible to really plan ahead and is too chaotic. 3 players is probably Max but 2 is sweet.
